# Stable Diffusion: Tips, Tricks, and Techniques

> AI image generation tools enable you to enter a text description and get an image in a flash. For creative pros, these tools can accelerate the creative process and help generate assets. For everyone else, these tools enable the effortless creation of images for presentations and reports—or just for fun. The open-source Stable Diffusion platform is known for its power, customizability, and ability

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques) | 6h 13m | 27K learners

### Introduction

#### [What is Stable Diffusion?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-is-stable-diffusion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-is-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=0)** - AI image generation is amazing. Enter some text, get an image, move on with your life. It's great. In this course, we're going to take a look at the finer details of how to write a good prompt. But in [[Stable Diffusion]], you are not limited to your prompt writing skills to get a good image. So we'll dig into image-to-image capabilities that let you start with a sketch, a photobash, or another AI rendering and use that as a prompt to get precisely the composition and image details that you want. We'll look at how to expand Stable Diffusion's functionality through extensions, and along the way, we'll talk about how you can run your own Stable Diffusion server, train Stable Diffusion to render things that it previously didn't know about and much, much more. If you've got a web browser, you're ready to go.

>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Stable Diffusion]] (4)
> **Speakers:** - ai (1)

#### [What can you do with Stable Diffusion?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-can-you-do-with-stable-diffusion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-can-you-do-with-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=0)** - You are going to learn how to use [[Stable Diffusion]], an AI image generator. Does this now mean that you will quickly be producing any image that you can imagine to your precise specifications? No. Before we go any further, I would like to suggest that you might want to start managing your expectations a little bit. Very often, getting a good usable image out of any AI generator can take a lot of time and work. There will be instances when you can't get the image that you want at all or can't get it to the level of quality that you want. We're in the first generation of this technology, and it's incredible, but it's not a perfect HAL 9000 level of AI power. That's good because it means your computer won't toss you out of an airlock. But it's important to know that you're going to have to coax the image that you want out of Stable Diffusion, sometimes, and sometimes that's a time consuming process. So why go through it? Because Stable Diffusion is an incredible piece of software and while it's not remotely intelligent, it does do a great [[Simulation]] of intelligence when it comes to making images. If you're running on a fast system, then Stable Diffusion can crank out pictures with astonishing speed. One of the first most immediate uses of Stable Diffusion is probably brainstorming and ideation. If you think maybe a design layout would look good with a photo of a person sitting at a Parisian cafe table, then you can quickly crank out an image of that scene. Sure, you could also quickly get such a shot
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-can-you-do-with-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=93)** from a stock photo site, but with Stable Diffusion you can experiment with more specific ideas. Maybe you specifically want a closeup with the person holding a hotdog, or maybe you'd like to experiment with a more stylized layout. And so you want to see some media besides photography, an oil painting or pastel drawing, or pencil sketch. Even if none of these images are suitable for final use, the ability to crank them out so quickly and to such a customized degree means that you can work with placeholder images that have much more value than what you might be able to access any other way. Character design for a video game or comic book, story boarding for a [[Video Production]], Stable Diffusion can tackle these tasks. Maybe one of the best things you can do with Stable Diffusion is communicate better. Perhaps you're an [[Microsoft Office|office]] manager who would like to convey some ideas for a new conference room. Stable Diffusion allows you to generate high quality visualizations that may not be perfect representations of the conference space you have, but that are close enough to allow you to convey ideas of mood, atmosphere, color treatments, and more. Stable Diffusion is not just useful for generating finished images. In fact, one of the best ways to incorporate it into your workflow might be to use it to generate assets that you combine with other processes. Maybe you composite Stable Diffusion elements into photos or natural media works. Maybe you use Stable Diffusion to generate textures for 3D software or to use in motion graphics. And then of course, Stable Diffusion is just fun. It doesn't satisfy the same things
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-can-you-do-with-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=188)** as generating work on your own, but as a fun way to explore images and ideas about images, you'll probably find that regular use often gives you a lot of laughs, and very often feelings of wonder and surprise. What can you use it for? I can't begin to imagine all of the things that you might do with Stable Diffusion, but as we work through this course, I expect you'll start getting a lot of ideas.

#### [What's different about Stable Diffusion?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-different-about-stable-diffusion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-different-about-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=0)** - I've been involved with computer graphics since the mid 1980s, so I've already gone through a few imaging revolutions. There was desktop publishing in the mid 1980s, the rise of digital image editing in the late 1980s, and then the ascension of digital photography in the late 1990s. Those developments truly were revolutionary and they fundamentally changed the industries that they affected. This AI imaging revolution is different. When digital tools first entered the world of [[Graphic Design]], photography, audio production, film and [[Video Production]], they provided digital versions of analog tools that professionals had always used. These new digital tools were faster, sometimes easier. And because they didn't require manual dexterity, anyone could produce technical results that previously required years of practice and development of muscle memory. But to apply these tools well, you still had to know a lot. In the case of design and photography, you had to know about composition, aesthetics, and a lot more. AI imaging is not a digital version of a tool that we have had before, it is something completely new. And what makes this a different kind of revolution is that you don't have to have any previous knowledge to get good results from these AI tools. Yes, in this course, we're going to go through instruction on how to drive the [[Stable Diffusion]] interface. And yes, it helps if you already know what a good image looks like, but these tools also come with aesthetics built in. If you take the time to generate enough images, you will get a good one
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-different-about-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=94)** regardless of any previous experience you might have had. At the time of this shooting, there are three major AI imaging tools available for use. [[DALL-E]], [[Midjourney]], and Stable Diffusion. Adobe has announced their Firefly technology and open betas are available. but it has not been formally released as of this production. All of these tools offer similar functionality. You write a prompt and the software generates an image. That image can be photorealistic or it can look like natural media. And in either case, it can be an image of something that no one has ever seen before. Under the hood, there are differences in the [[Algorithms]] that are used, but fundamentally they share some common traits. They were all trained by analyzing huge quantities of images, and all four have very sophisticated human language processors in them. Those two things work together to create what we generally refer to as an AI image generator. There are some differences. DALL-E and Midjourney both [[Microsoft Excel|excel]] at photorealism, but Midjourney is also capable of a particular Midjourney look which skews towards atmospheric fantasy illustration. By default, Stable Diffusion sits somewhere between photorealism and illustration, but Stable Diffusion also allows you to swap out its core set of trained images, making it possible to give Stable Diffusion new imaging capabilities and entirely different looks. You can, in fact, add a data model that makes Stable Diffusion render images that look very much like Midjourney's fantasy style images.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-different-about-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=188)** We'll dig into all of that later in this course. DALL-E's data set is more curated than Stable Diffusion's default data set. This means that you can more easily create not safe for work type images in Stable Diffusion. If you're in an education environment, DALL-E's curation might be a better choice for you because it's harder to create potentially offensive images. On the other hand, you can easily find safe for work models for Stable Diffusion. Access to these systems differs. Stable Diffusion, DALL-E, and Firefly are all accessed through a web browser. While you access Midjourney through the Discord server. Firefly will likely see integration into Adobe's apps and there are already plug-ins and add-ons for integrating Stable Diffusion into Photoshop. What's more, you can get standalone applications for the macOS and iOS that allow you to run Stable Diffusion as standalone apps or you can create your own Stable Diffusion server on a Mac, [[Windows]], or [[Linux]] machine. There are questions of morality around the practice of training data sets off of images that don't belong to you. Adobe's Firefly doesn't suffer from these concerns because it was trained off of public domain images and Adobe's own stock photo service. Perhaps one of the most significant differences between Stable Diffusion and its competitors is that you can use Stable Diffusion for free. Where DALL-E, Midjourney, and Firefly ultimately require payment. This is because of one of the other big differences with Stable Diffusion which is that it is open source software. This is why you have the option to run it
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-different-about-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=280)** on your own computer for free, but it's open source nature also means that there's a huge Stable Diffusion community that is constantly pushing out new data models, plugins, and lots more. In addition to being able to change its data models, Stable Diffusion offers some other features that its competitors lack. For example, you can train Stable Diffusion so that it knows how to make images of specific people or things that you want to render. Stable diffusion includes an image to image feature which allows you to use a simple sketch in conjunction with a text prompt to create an image. This is sometimes the only way to get the precise composition that you want, and so it's a great tool to have at your disposal. As far as which system is best, they're all amazing. And you don't have to wonder, you can get free time with all four systems and make some decisions for yourself. All of these platforms take time to master but it's not a waste of time to invest study in one even if you ultimately end up favoring another because much of the skill and understanding that you develop in one are applicable to all four. I love Stable Diffusion's feature set. I don't think you sacrifice image quality choosing any one of these systems over another, and it's hard to beat free. So I think you've made the right choice in deciding to learn Stable Diffusion. Let's get to it.

#### [How can you access Stable Diffusion?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/how-can-you-access-stable-diffusion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/how-can-you-access-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=0)** - As mentioned in the last video [[Stable Diffusion]] is open source software. One of the practical upshots of that is that there are many different ways that you can access Stable Diffusion. The easiest way is via the web by pointing your web browser to a publicly available Stable Diffusion node. This is simply a machine or network of machines running Stable Diffusion and providing a web-based interface to the software. [StableDiffusionweb.com](https://StableDiffusionweb.com) is probably the best place to start. In addition to access to the software that site includes a lot of very good tutorial information and background as well as technical details on how Stable Diffusion works. [[Hugging Face]] is a name you're probably going to get more familiar with if you dig much deeper into Stable Diffusion. Hugging Face builds itself as a community and [[Data Science]] platform on a journey to advance and democratize AI through open source and open science. Depending on how far you go, you may find yourself downloading a lot of code and models and libraries from Hugging Face. In the meantime, you can use their Stable Diffusion server. Mage.space also provides a front end to Stable Diffusion. They offer free access with some limitations. You'll see ads and you won't be running on their fastest [[Hardware]]. For a monthly fee, you can gain access to more features at various tiers. These features include access to additional data models, no ads, faster hardware. By the time you watch this video
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/how-can-you-access-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=93)** some of these options may have gone away others may have turned up. A search engine will quickly lead you to a usable, Stable Diffusion site. Because it's open source, you can download Stable Diffusion and install it on your own computer. This is a complex process that we'll discuss in the next movie. Fortunately, some people have rolled the Stable Diffusion software into normal applications that can be easily installed and used like any other day-to-day app. For example, Draw Things is an iPad app that is a complete Stable Diffusion install including the option to download multiple data models. At a minimum, it's going to chew up four to five gigabytes of storage, but it runs entirely on the iPad and presents a very nice interface. Diffusion B is the same thing, but for Mac OS. [AllThingsAI.com](https://AllThingsAI.com) is a good source for learning about what applications are out there for your particular OS. For now, hitting a site like [StableDiffusionWeb.com](https://StableDiffusionWeb.com) is certainly the easiest way to get up and running.

#### [Installing Stable Diffusion locally](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Installing [[Stable Diffusion]] on your own [[Hardware]] offers a number of advantages, the most significant of which is speed. If you've got a fast video card then you can get extremely quick rendering times. Here you can see I'm rendering images at an average speed of five or six seconds each. For iterating and generating large batches, this is great. By comparison, using an online site can sometimes take up to a minute per image. Now, a lot of my speed gain when running locally comes simply from the fact that I don't have to share processor time with lots of other users because I'm running on my own hardware. The downside to this is that to get really fast rendering, you've got to have an expensive video card. Stable Diffusion is open source software, but at the time of this shooting, there is no official release of it of any kind. Instead, a number of enthusiasts have created and released distributions, which you can install for free. There may be commercial releases one day, but at the time of this shooting, there are just a few main players. A distribution called AUTOMATIC1111 is the most popular and most widely supported. It's available from a [[GitHub]] repository and you can find instructions there for installation or you can use the installation guide that I've written, which is available in the course notes or you can find all sorts of guides online. Installation does require the use of the command line, and it requires you to install a number of dependencies. If my mention of either of those things makes you nervous, then installing Stable Diffusion may not be for you. AUTOMATIC1111, which you see here, presents a solid, decent, easy to use interface, but it's not glamorous by any means.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980&t=96)** Straight out of the box, It's got a very full feature set, but maybe the best thing about it is that it is extensible through the use of downloadable plugins or extensions. As research leads to new AI imaging breakthroughs, it seems like an AUTOMATIC1111 extension that exploits those breakthroughs is available almost immediately. Invoke AI is another distribution, it provides a slicker looking interface and an environment that feels more like an image editing application than a browser-based server side tool. Infinite undoes, a built-in gallery for browsing images that you've made and excellent in painting and out painting tools, among many other things, make this a really nice place to work. At the time of this shooting, the feature set is quite a bit smaller than AUTOMATIC1111's and there are no extensions. If you've got the capable hardware, you can install both of these and switch between them, but it's best not to have them both running at the same time. On the server itself, both of these systems show up as a simple terminal window, where you can see what activity the system is currently executing. But that's all you see. All configuration and management is implemented through a web-based interface. I have included PDFs with installation instructions for both AUTOMATIC1111 and Invoke AI on either the Mac or [[Windows]]. If you're a [[Linux]] user, you can also install both of these systems. Since the Mac OS is basically Unix, you should be able to follow the Mac's installation instructions with no problem. If you run into an issue, a search engine should lead you to plenty of installation documents and videos. There's one detail of installation
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980&t=191)** that I found it difficult to find online documentation for, so I'm going to cover it here. Both AUTOMATIC1111 and Invoke AI are launched by running a Windows batch file. As explained in my Windows installation document, you can edit that batch file. Here, I'm opening up the batch file for AUTOMATIC1111, and if I add this --listen command to the end of this line, save this, and then restart AUTOMATIC1111, I can now access this instance of Stable Diffusion from any machine on my network. Here I am back on my Mac in my web browser, I know the IP address of my Windows machine where Stable Diffusion is running, so I enter that followed by a colon and 7860, which is the port number that Stable Diffusion listens to. I enter all that and here I am. I can do this from my iPhone if I want to or any other device on my LAN. Invoke AI works the same way and you'll find instructions in its own launch batch file. If you want to access your Stable Diffusion machine from outside of your house, you'll need to open the appropriate ports in your server's firewall, and you'll need to set up port forwarding on your router, which is what I'm doing here, so that traffic on port 7860 gets sent to the server machine on your network. Invoke AI uses port 9090. Instructions for all of that can be found online, but note that not all routers offer a port forwarding feature. So if you want this capability, you may end up having to upgrade your router. There's another option for running AUTOMATIC1111,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980&t=285)** and it's ideal if you don't have a fast video card. [[Google]] Colab is a Google service that allows you to run [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] applications on computers hosted by Google. A lot of work has been done to make it very simple to get AUTOMATIC1111 running in what's called a Colab notebook. You can often get much better performance with Google Colab than you can get from other publicly available servers, and if you don't have a fast video card, then Google Colab will almost certainly be faster than what you can do at home. You can do quite a bit of work for free, though if you get serious about using it, you may want to subscribe. Google provides several pricing options, which give you increasing levels of rendering quantity, and access to faster and faster hardware. Going into the details of Colab is way beyond the scope of this course, but with a simple web search, you can easily find instructions for installing and using AUTOMATIC1111 on Colab. In this course, you're mostly going to see me using my own server because I want this kind of speed, and you're mostly going to see me using AUTOMATIC1111, which I'm going to be referring to as Stable Diffusion. If you're using an online instance of Stable Diffusion, any of the ones discussed in the last video, that's fine. You'll see equivalent controls to what I'm using but the interface to them will probably be different and they might use different labels and names for their various controls and sliders. I know this sounds like a lot of information, but honestly, we're just getting started with the topic of installing Stable Diffusion.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/installing-stable-diffusion-locally?u=76281980&t=377)** We'll be returning to this subject in a later chapter. It's important to understand this odd, ad hoc, open source nature that Stable Diffusion has. From time to time, you are going to see out in the world what profess to be new AI imaging tools but which, upon closer examination, you can recognize as just being re-skinned versions of Stable Diffusion. Being able to recognize that will help you better understand if particular [[Microsoft Products|products]] are things that you actually want to investigate.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=0)** - We're finally ready to actually have [[Stable Diffusion]] make us a picture. Because Stable Diffusion is running in a web browser, it's easy for these sites to customize the [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]] to exactly what they want. And some sites opt to shield the user from too many options. Here's what the interface looks like if you install the Stable Diffusion software on your own machine. This is what I'm going to be using throughout this course. > **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=94)** somewhere in my interface. Also, my video card is very fast. If it turns out that at some point in this course I'm accessing a control that you don't see in the Stable Diffusion that you're using, then you'll need to go try another site if you want to follow along with whatever I'm talking about then. So we're ready. We just need to enter a prompt. A prompt is simply a description of the image that we want. Since this will be the inaugural image in our AI image-making experience together, why don't we ask for a photo of the inauguration of a giant robot as emperor of the universe. That ought to keep the AI very happy. Before you hit Generate, take a moment to consider this prompt. I didn't just say the inauguration of. I started with a photo, as opposed to an oil painting or a sketch or concept art of. This is sometimes referred to as the frame of the prompt. We're specifying the media we want. The [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] is pretty simple. Let's hit Generate and see what we get. I'll buy that. Now, when you hit Generate, you're not going to get the same image. And I'll explain why in a later movie. For now, let's do a little more work. First of all, a prompt doesn't always yield a great image. So if your Stable Diffusion client allows it, dial up the batch count or number of images or whatever your client calls it to something higher. I'm going to set mine to three and then hit Generate.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-stable-diffusion?u=76281980&t=188)** When it's done, I have three images and this little thumbnail collection of all of them. The reason it's better to render multiples is that there will be times when a prompt yields a really bad result. If you only see one result, you might be tempted to rework your prompt when your prompt is actually fine on the next generation. So don't ever judge a prompt by only one result. I'll sometimes set mine to generate 30, then I'll browse through them later. Obviously, if you're on a very slow system, that may not be practical. So spend some time generating a bunch of images off of this one prompt, just so you can get a feel for how much variation there can be from one generation to another. Along the way, if you end up with an image you like, you should have a Download button somewhere in your interface which will allow you to download a copy. By default, Stable Diffusion renders a 512 by 512 image, so downloading one is very speedy and doesn't take up much storage. Coming up with the right prompt is the single most important step in getting good results from Stable Diffusion. So next, we'll look at prompt-writing in more detail.

### 2. Digging Deeper into Stable Diffusion Prompts

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What does a prompt do?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-does-a-prompt-do?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-does-a-prompt-do?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] If you've played with the [[ChatGPT]] bot, then you know that there are very good AI language models out there. They don't actually know any language. They're just statistical models that are very good at predicting what the next [[Microsoft Word|word]] should be in any given sentence. There are also a critical part of an image generation tool because that prompt that you write in an image generator has to be interpreted somehow. Like all machine learning tools, [[Stable Diffusion]] was trained on a dataset. Specifically, it was trained on a dataset acquired from a company called LAION. In this case, this was a dataset consisting of images with text descriptions. Over a billion image-text pairs at a training cost of hundreds of thousands of dollars were used to train a model for Stable Diffusion. For each image, Stable Diffusion did a bunch of analysis and stored some resulting data. None of that data would be of any use if there weren't that text associated with it. After all, it doesn't matter if the machine knows how to draw a dog if you have no way to ask it for a drawing of a dog. These image generation systems are so useful because of their natural language interface. This is a number line. You should recognize this from elementary school. A number line isn't complicated, but it is a clever way of showing a spatial relationship for a number. Here's a Cartesian coordinate grid. I can mark places on this grid using pairs of numbers. With three numbers, I can define a point in 3D space. We're going to come back to this in a minute. An AI language model converts text into numbers, a process called tokenizing, because different bits
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-does-a-prompt-do?u=76281980&t=96)** of text end up represented by specific numeric [[Tokens]]. I'm not talking about ASCII. A token is not a number representing a specific letter. Tokens can be words or other language constructs. As you type a prompt, Automatic1111 shows you count of how many tokens you've used against your maximum token count of 75. That's this business right here. I've installed an extension in Stable Diffusion that will show me how text gets tokenized. I enter a prompt like "A woman sitting on a park bench eating a sandwich wearing a yellow dress on a fall afternoon," and I tokenize and I can see that it considers this sentence to have 21 tokens. And here they are listed out. After a little bit of experimenting, I have found that the same words always get the same tokens. During Stable Diffusion's training, different qualities and aspects of an image are also assigned numbers of various kinds. As we've seen, numbers can be represented as locations in space. So one way to think of Stable Diffusion's data model is that it's a big multidimensional space with specific data stored in specific locations within that space. Different text tokens lead you to different locations in that space. And in each location, you find data related to specific qualities and aspects of an image or part of an image. Now this is a very simplified explanation. One thing we haven't talked about is what "oil painting" might refer to. Dog is pretty simple to understand,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-does-a-prompt-do?u=76281980&t=188)** but what visual qualities does "oil painting" reference? The answer is that we don't know. An artificial neural network, like the one driving Stable Diffusion, is very good at recognizing patterns, subtle, complex patterns. We don't know what patterns it's picking up on when it's trained. But as you can see from using it, Stable Diffusion is very good at equating text tokens with specific image particulars and qualities to create specific imagery.

#### [Stable Diffusion seeds](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-seeds?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-seeds?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you saw in the last video, [[Stable Diffusion]] generates images by using numbers associated with text to identify specific data that can be used to diffuse an image out of a pattern of noise. So one of the things that Stable Diffusion has to do when it generates an image is to create an initial field of noise. It bases this random noise pattern on a numeric seed. Different seed numbers yield different noise patterns and every noise pattern results in a different image even when the prompt doesn't change. That's why if both you and I enter the same prompt into our separate systems we'll most likely get different images. In fact, if you come back later and enter the same prompt into your own system, you'll likely get a different image than what you got the first time. In automatic 1111, there's a seed field right down here. If you're using a different Stable Diffusion system it should have a control for seed somewhere in its interface. Mine is currently set to minus one, which indicates to the system that it should generate a random number. If I click this recycle button, it sets the seed to whatever the seed was for the last image that I generated. By the way, you can browse all sorts of parameters for the most recently generated images right here and here you can see the seed. I can also enter a seed here by hand. If I click the die button, it sets me back to random which shows up as minus one.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-seeds?u=76281980&t=96)** Manipulating the seed is very useful and we'll be doing that throughout this course. I wanted you to see right away why if you're following along, entering the same prompt, configuring the same parameters, you're probably not getting the same images that I am. If you are, then you have just experienced a freakish level of coincidence because what has happened, is that your computer has generated the same random number as mine at the same time.

#### [Stable Diffusion batches and pixel counts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-batches-and-pixel-counts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-batches-and-pixel-counts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we dive into lots of image making, I want to say that if you're someone who has a tendency towards perfectionism or if you're someone who strives for great efficiency, then you might find yourself a little uncomfortable working with [[Stable Diffusion]] or any AI image generator. The fact is there's a huge amount of trial and error and luck involved in using these systems. You are very rarely going to sit down, enter a prompt, and get the image that you want. It's very likely that you're going to have to generate dozens of images before you hit on something that you can consider as a starting point for lots of additional refinement. Working with Stable Diffusion is a lot like fishing or mushroom hunting. You've got to be patient and you've got to be ready to try lots of things. For that reason, before we even start writing a prompt, I'm going to increase the batch count from one to four. Now, every time I click the Generate button, Stable Diffusion will generate four different images. You could also set the batch size to four. This would also yield four images, but in a different way. Note that not all Stable Diffusion distributions offer a batch size option. Here's the window showing my server activity. In my client, if I set batch count to four, enter a prompt and hit Generate, you can see on the server that what happens is that Stable Diffusion executes four separate image generation processes one after another. If I set the batch count back to one and increase the batch size to four, and then hit Generate on the server,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-batches-and-pixel-counts?u=76281980&t=95)** there's only one image generation, but it renders four images. The difference has to do with memory usage on the video card. Increasing batch size may get you results a little faster, but it will use more VRAM on your graphics card. Increasing the batch count will use less RAM but take a tiny bit longer. However, using less RAM means you can possibly render images with higher pixel counts. My recommendation would be to leave batch size set to one and increase batch count. Work that way for now, and if you one day want to do some experimenting to see if there's a notable performance difference, then maybe you'll find that adjusting batch size works better for you in some circumstances. By default, most stable diffusion systems generate results that are 512 by 512 pixels. If that sounds very small to you, that's because it is. If you're hoping to generate 20 megapixel images to print out on a large format printer, you're not going to be able to do that with the results that come straight out of the system. You can, of course, upsize, and Stable Diffusion includes its own upsizing tools, and we'll look into those later. As you can see, Stable Diffusions size sliders don't go up very high. And if you try to crank them up this far, you may find your results get really wonky. Maximum size can vary depending on which model you're using, how much memory you have on your graphics card, and some other factors that we'll talk more about later. A handy tool for figuring out what to put in your width and height fields here is [calculateaspectratio.com](https://calculateaspectratio.com). This lets me easily figure out pixel dimensions
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/stable-diffusion-batches-and-pixel-counts?u=76281980&t=189)** for a given aspect ratio. So for example, if I want 16 by 9 and I don't want to go over 512, I can put 512 in here, and I find out that the correct height for 16 by 9 is 288. So I could come back here and set my height to 288. That said, you can go above 512. Let's say that, with some experimenting, I'd found that actually I'm okay up to 1,000 pixels width. So then my height would be 563, I'm going to round up there. So that's a really great tool for figuring out aspect ratios as you go.

For now, it's important to understand that Stable Diffusion data models fall into two categories, models based on the original 1.4 model and models based on the newer 2.0 model. Stable Diffusion's prompts behave very differently depending on whether you're using 1.4-based models or 2.0-based models. We'll talk about the differences later. For right now, know that 1.4 series models are capable of generating not-safe-for-work content. So if you're working in a sensitive environment, you might want to be careful with your prompts. So I could, if I wanted to, pick one of these, grab its seed number, throw it in and keep heading in that direction. This is the Stable Diffusion prompt writing process. Let's add just a couple more things, and then I'm going to ask you to go off on your own. I'm going to say I want detailed, I'm going to add 8K, I'm going to add photorealistic. These are all things that will get me a different type of image. And here we go. Nothing, no great profound discoveries here. This isn't bad. But they didn't affect a huge change, and sometimes it's like that. So what I would like you to do now is go play on your own with trying to come up with a good shot of a woman in business casual clothing in front of Stonehenge. If you think you've got a better idea for raising some money for this product that we're going to try and sell, that's great, follow those ideas. Work with your negative prompt, your normal prompt. And I also want you to play with changing the order of things. As far as Stable Diffusion is concerned, the closer things are to the front of the prompt the more important they are, the more it's going to pay attention to those things.
>
> **[10:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/prompt-basics?u=76281980&t=657)** So maybe try throwing photorealistic up at the front, maybe move cloudy sky to the back, see what happens. Think of some other keywords, put them in there, and see what you can come up with.

#### [Questions to answer when writing prompts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/questions-to-answer-when-writing-prompts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/questions-to-answer-when-writing-prompts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Working with an AI image generator can be a little weird because you set out to make an image and immediately find yourself facing a writing problem, namely the problem of trying to think of words that describe the scene that you want. If you're someone who spends more time image making than [[Microsoft Word|word]] crafting, this can be a frustrating barrier. Making all of this more difficult is the fact that the creators of [[Stable Diffusion]] have never published any documentation. There is no list of keywords that were in the training set, and there are a lot of possible words out there in the world. Something that can make describing a scene easier is to ask yourself some questions about the image that you have in mind. The answers to these questions can go straight into your prompt. First, what is your subject? Now, that may sound like a simple question, but consider something like a post-apocalyptic cyberpunk urban adventurer scene. There are a lot of evocative, descriptive words in there, but there's not actually a subject. A subject is a thing you can point at and reference specifically. Next, what is the style of the image? Is it a photo, an oil painting, a 3D rendering? Style doesn't have to be a medium. For example, maybe you are looking for a pulp novel cover, a movie poster, a xeroxed flyer. I'm using the word style pretty broadly here. Photo versus oil painting. Those are two very different mediums while tarot card is a very specific look using a very specific medium. Note also that these examples that I'm giving you are just examples. These are not in any way meant to represent a full list of the words you can choose to use to answer this question.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/questions-to-answer-when-writing-prompts?u=76281980&t=95)** That's true for all of these other questions, too. Think about defining a camera angle or point of view. Should your subject be seen from above or below? I expect you're going to find that Stable Diffusion does not respond as well to camera directions as you would like. If you were picturing a low angle, looking up at a subject, writing that specific thing into your prompt probably won't get it for you. Don't spend too much time trying to [[Jetpack Compose|compose]] your shot and set your camera angle using the prompt. It's very limited, and there are many other tools that give you precise control over composition, camera angle and framing. In fact, you may not need any camera angle descriptions in your prompt at all, but this is still a good question to ask yourself to help you refine your own idea of what you want and what the final image should look like. What's the background? Where is your subject? There's a big difference between a photo of a girl on a submarine and a photo of a girl at the zoo. Simply stating where your subject is is usually enough for Stable Diffusion to create some kind of background detail. You can also say, standing in front of whatever, the pyramids, a station wagon, a soccer field. Do you envision particular lighting, dramatic lighting, dark, moody lighting, fall light, high key, whatever? A lighting prompt may be the thing that has the greatest impact on the atmosphere in your scene. It might also be the part of the prompt that lets Stable Diffusion know what time of day your scene is set in. Do you envision a particular color palette, black and white, pastel, saturated? You can even specify a particular tint or wash such as a blue tint.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/questions-to-answer-when-writing-prompts?u=76281980&t=189)** You don't have to answer every one of these questions for every image, but these are definitely categories of modifiers that Stable Diffusion will likely respond to. Perhaps more importantly, answering these questions will help you better envision the image that you want. Also, when writing your prompt, you do not have to include the answers to all of these questions in this order. As we saw earlier, keyword order has a big impact on your final image. Oh, also, Stable Diffusion is not case sensitive. Upper case doesn't seem to matter at all. The answers to these questions are not the only things that might go into your prompt. Stable Diffusion responds well to a lot of words that don't fit into any of these questions. Remember, the image you want is sitting out there somewhere in the virtual space defined by your Stable Diffusion model, and you're trying to navigate to it. Any word you put in your prompt can help steer or nudge the system to the image that you want. For that reason, there are a lot of extra modifiers that people often add to a prompt. For example, you might see people adding things like 8K or 4K or photorealistic or HDR. These all help to increase the level of detail in the image, sometimes. If they're after a photorealistic portrait, then they might add detailed skin in addition to the photorealistic keyword. Octane render is another one that you'll see a lot. If that doesn't mean anything to you, know that Octane is the name of a 3D rendering product. Images rendered by Octane are known for a particular look, especially high detail. Remember, the text descriptions that Stable Diffusion knows are things that were
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/questions-to-answer-when-writing-prompts?u=76281980&t=282)** scraped from the web alongside images. So, think of how someone might describe an image or the subject of an image in say a stock photo library or on Etsy or in a product catalog. Think about if you were posting an image to the web and you wanted it to be found in a search. What words might you want to associate with that image? Some of those words would be descriptors of the content of the image. Some would be descriptors of the style. Some might even just be loose associations, things you add to, again, nudge the search. Maybe you add Middle Earth to a prompt to get a pastoral, moody feel, even if you're not specifically creating a fantasy image. I've given you these lists of words which are hardly comprehensive for their questions, but as you will see in a little bit, there are other places to find examples of effective prompt terms. Before we get to those, let's get back to some imaging.

#### [Using CFG scale](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-cfg-scale?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-cfg-scale?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Language is a slippery thing. Different people can interpret the same sentence in multiple ways, and the AI in [[Stable Diffusion]] is no different. However, unlike that annoying coworker who always misinterprets you, stable diffusion allows you to specify how freely it should interpret your prompt. You do that with this control labeled CFG Scale, or Classifier Free Guidance. This is a great example of a parameter name that was lifted from the underlying theory, but that doesn't mean much in the real world. Other interfaces may show you something else. mage.space, for example, has these guidance scale buttons down here, unless you click this Advanced Mode switch, and in which case, it then turns into this scale with some hints down here at the bottom. Low, normal, Strict, Very Strict, Insane. What that's referring to is how tightly it pays attention to your prompt. Let's look at another example. Here in dreamlike.art, if you turn on the advanced mode, you get a guidance scale. They drop the classifier free bit, and they give you a recommended value of 14. That's different than what's in AUTOMATIC1111 and mage.space. They describe it as how much to prioritize prompt over creativity. I'm not crazy about this definition, because the AI is not actually creative, but maybe the best way to think about it is just this [[Microsoft Word|word]] guidance, which is not shown in AUTOMATIC1111, but CFG, Classifier Free Guidance Scale.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-cfg-scale?u=76281980&t=93)** If you mouse over it, it shows that to you. What they mean by guidance is how much will the prompt guide the de-noising process that leads to a final image. If I turn this way down, then the prompt will have very little effect on the process of creating an image. If I turn it way up, then in theory, it will have a tremendous effect. So as you can see here in stable diffusion, the scale goes from one to 30, and defaults to seven. Over there in the dreamlike.art, theirs defaults to 14. mage.space doesn't have numbers. Oh, yes, they do. Their numbers show up when you mouse over these spots here. So they default to seven and a half. Now you may think, "Why would I want it to not do what's in my prompt? Why don't I just turn this up all the way?" Well, let's take a look. I've got the prompt that I've been using here that has been generating messed up faces and getting me some usable results sometimes, and not other times. Oops, my Batch count was set to one. Let's crank that up to four, and try that again. So I've got the CFG Scale set to seven, so it's at its default setting, and it's generating images very much like the ones we've been seeing before. I actually kind of like that pose and that composition. Interesting in black and white, I hadn't considered that before. So if I turn this down to one, its lowest setting, and generate four more images, now we're having very little guidance from the prompt. The AI is just free to do whatever it wants. So here, it left the person out altogether.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-cfg-scale?u=76281980&t=188)** It's been doing that even with it set to seven. It's sometimes putting in a woman, it's sometimes not, and I can't even really tell what she's doing when she is. I don't even know, that's some kind of bipedal creature wearing a poncho, it looks like. Here we got a couple of people that barely look like people and a wall. So it's pretty out there. So let's take this up. Let's just go all the way, we'll go to 30. Yeah, and now, curiously enough, as I've said follow the prompt more, it feels like it's being more creative. I don't know how it got to here. I've lost Stonehenge altogether. These are not photorealistic. So it's just gone, it's gone way out of the park on either ends of the scale. Let's say instead of seven, we do something like 20. And even this is looking pretty fantastical. In my experience, sticking around seven is usually the best thing, but you hear that, and it tends to make you forget about this control, and sometimes this control really will make the difference between you getting an image that you want and not. I can't think of an instance where I've turned it down and ended up with better results,
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-cfg-scale?u=76281980&t=280)** but turning it up, sometimes even as high as nine, will sometimes make it recognize something in my prompt that it was ignoring. If I've asked her to hold a football, or something like that, and the football's not showing up, I can turn up the CFG Scale, and then the football will show up. An interesting thing about teaching this course, you may be saying, "Well, why don't you show me that example?" Because there's this whole random element. There's no guarantee that if I try that example right now, it's going to work for you. You just got to, or it's going to show up that way, you've just got to take my word for it. I like this image. This is an interesting color palette. Stonehenge has gotten a little wonky, but I like the idea of her wearing a skirt that looks like stone. So that's maybe something to remember as we move forward, that that was a cool idea. If you're trying to generate something that stable diffusion doesn't know about, a duck riding on a dragon, something that it definitely doesn't have a picture of, then increasing CFG Scale probably will have more of an impact. It needs more guidance at that point. Note also that the model you are using can impact how effective the CFG Scale slider is. So don't forget about CFG Scale. It's not a magic bullet. If you're having bad results, turning it up is not going to suddenly fix your image unless it does, and there will be times when it does, but never by turning it up very far. It's just another parameter to try as you go, as you're iterating, as you're generating lots of batches.

#### [Prompt weighting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/prompt-weighting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/prompt-weighting?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] So I'm continuing to toil through this prompt writing of trying to get a woman standing in front of Stonehenge. Here's a purse on a hip, sort of, but it's just missed it entirely and nevermind that she's looking away and so on and so forth. I've got closeup photo, I need to say that. Woman in business casual clothing standing in front of Stonehenge. That needs to come before hands on hips, but I can still lend weight to a particular token or set of [[Tokens]] in my prompts so that [[Stable Diffusion]] will add more emphasis to it and possibly stand a better chance of putting it into my image. >
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/prompt-weighting?u=76281980&t=187)** and this number syntax each increment in tenths of the a unit. Another thing you can do is just put parentheses around something. Let's beef up the cloudy sky. If I put one set of parentheses around a token, then that increases the strength by 0.1. If I put two sets around it, that increases it by 0.2. Let's see what that does. That's a common syntax in most instances. In a lot of instances of Stable Diffusion, there won't be a negative prompt box. Instead to get a negative prompt, you'll write a token and put square brackets around it. The greater the number of square brackets, the greater the increase in negativity. So that is a more dramatic sky, but it's coming at the cost of Stonehenge. It's dropped out of the picture some. So I'm going to take some of the emphasis off the cloudy sky because it was plainly making things too much about the cloudy sky. So what we might need to do here, since we want more drama on the cloudy sky, but we're losing Stonehenge, is let's in increase the Stonehenge strength. We keep losing it. Let's make sure it doesn't drop out. There we go. Hey, look, there was a woman with hands on her hips and a good strong Stonehenge and a dramatic sky. It's starting to come together. Look at that. I'm not crazy about the expression of her face but at least she has a face. It's a non-disfigured face, so we can work with that. Stonehenge has picked up leopard spots but we might be able to do something about that. This isn't bad either, so it's working. Combining these, wow, look at that arm. That's great.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/prompt-weighting?u=76281980&t=279)** Well, arms and hands are as flaky as as faces are sometimes. We'll fix those later. Anyway, you can see that changing weights is making a difference, finding a balance of different weights so that one thing doesn't take over too much. That's a tricky bit that you've got to play with. This process that I'm going through here, again, the reason I didn't stop the recording and try starting something else hoping that I would get a good result right away is that I just rarely get a good result right away. This is what you have to do, is just iterate through this process of playing with weights. I use weights in just about every prompt that I make. They're very important. You need to practice with them. If you're using something other than AUTOMATIC1111, then look for a help file that tells you the syntax for indicating weights.

Perhaps most significantly for some people is that two series models do not respond as well to listing specific artist styles in a prompt. That doesn't mean you should ignore two series models. Just know that they're going to require a little study to be able to use well.

#### [Artist names and rendering styles](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/artist-names-and-rendering-styles?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/artist-names-and-rendering-styles?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are currently a few prototype methods for creating animation in [[Stable Diffusion]]. More accurately, what you're seeing here is a form of rotoscoping using Stable Diffusion. [[Video Production]] is beyond the scope of this course, but keep an eye out for a course devoted to using Stable Diffusion for animation and 3D. If you look at other prompt writing guides or at sample prompts that are out there on the web, you're going to see something that I haven't mentioned yet, and that is the practice of including a specific artist name in your prompt. Stable Diffusion trained itself by analyzing gobs of images that all had text associated with them, and very often that text was the name of the creator of that associated image. So, the system ended up learning the rendering styles of many different visual artists in many different media. Consequently, it knows how to create images that look like things specific artists made. And you can often get those looks simply by adding the associated artist's name to your prompt. I would encourage you to not do this. Ultimately, this is a personal decision, but I think including artist names in your prompts is a bad idea and I think this for a few reasons. First of all, it's creepy. There are already a lot of discussions out there as well as lawsuits about whether it was right to train the AI on people's works without compensating those people, or even allowing them to opt out of the training process. I can argue that when either way, but when it comes to deciding to specifically
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/artist-names-and-rendering-styles?u=76281980&t=93)** create an image so that it looks like it was painted by a particular person who is not you, when you have decided to create an image with premeditated deception in it, when you have decided to basically create a forgery, at that point I think you're on shaky moral ground. But also, why would you want to do this? If you want to replicate the work of someone else, go buy a paint by numbers set. You'll achieve the same thing, but along the way you'll experience actual mark making. You'll build some mechanical skill and maybe get some insight into why the artist originally built a work in the way that they did, and you'll get away from your computer for a while. When you use an AI to generate a work in the style of a specific artist, you are inherently creating an inferior work. Because the AI is never going to do as good a job as that person would've done because the AI is too stupid and because it's not them. So you're immediately handicapping yourself and you're basically creating what can be called at best a parody. I'm a photography instructor and one of the things I've noticed over the years is that beginning photographers often take a photo because they recognize it as something that looks like a photo. They spot lighting or composition or subject matter that is similar to things that they've seen in other photos. Learning to do that and see that way is a necessary step in becoming a good photographer, but better photographers take photos because they have an emotional response to something, and so they said about trying to figure out how to capture that emotional response using photographic vocabulary.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/artist-names-and-rendering-styles?u=76281980&t=186)** From time to time, they create something that doesn't look like a photo anyone has seen before but it works. And in those times, they move the medium forward. I think a lot of people choose to include artists' names in their prompts because they don't actually know what they like or what makes a good image. They just know that an image that looks like one made by so-and-so looks cool. Rather than throwing in an artist name because that makes an image that you like, Learn why you like the images you like. Learn what makes an image work and then start trying to find prompts that achieve that type of image. At that point, you are not being deceptive, you are not stealing someone else's time and effort, your own visual senses growing and developing, and who knows, maybe you'll end up being one of the people who move AI image making into the world of fine art.

>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/sampling-and-steps?u=76281980&t=94)** The sampling steps field lets you specify how many of these noise removal passes Stable Diffusion will make when it renders. >
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/sampling-and-steps?u=76281980&t=187)** At this point, she's starting to look a little too cartoony although another rendering at 150 steps may not. If you had been standing here while I was doing it though, you would've noticed something which is the more steps I have the longer it takes to render. The higher this sampling steps number is, the more you're going to be waiting. So if you're on slow [[Hardware]] you really don't want to be cranking up the steps. This is why it's nice to figure out what are the least number of steps that you need to get an image that you like. If you can work at that level, you're going to be able to iterate faster. You can't get too near it or something. I don't know, I really like this. It's not something I had thought of before and so it's a great example of generative imaging helping me brainstorm and come up with something I wouldn't have thought of necessarily on my own. So same prompt, same negative prompt, same number of steps, different sampler. We haven't talked about samplers yet. It turns out there are many different [[Algorithms]] that Stable Diffusion can use for removing noise from that initial noise field that it starts with.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/sampling-and-steps?u=76281980&t=280)** These are called sampling methods and most Stable Diffusion instances provide you with a choice of samplers. In AUTOMATIC1111, we get this big long list. Sampling choice can be overwhelming because there can be a lot of options and the names are meaningless and don't you already have enough parameters and settings to worry about? The good news is that most of the time your choice of samplers is not going to make that big a difference. Check this out. This grid shows you that same prompt that I just showed you rendered in these different samplers at these different steps. So columns are steps 20, 30, 40, and 50 and rows are samplers. So you can see that Euler yields something very different than what I was just showing you and if you increase the number of steps too far you start getting a second Stonehenge in the sky. As we look over these though, we can see that there's really not a huge difference. They're all coming up with the same layout. They all, some of them start to generate weird artifacts at too many steps. DDIM has this woman looking away in at 20 steps but kind of coming back to what we were thinking of. PLMS looks a little too contrasty to me down here. I know these are small on your screen, I am going to make this document available for you to download so you can look at it up close. But even at this thumbnail size, you should be able to tell there's not a huge difference. That's good news. It turns out that most of the time, your choice of samplers is not going to make that big a difference. If you want to do some experimenting
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/sampling-and-steps?u=76281980&t=372)** what you'll probably find is that some samplers exhibit something called convergence. After a certain number of steps, their images don't change. Other samplers never converge. Their images always alter. Some samplers like DDIM produce a useful image in far fewer steps than others, though it might not be as nice as what another sampler will do in more steps. If you're working with a slower system then you can prototype a prompt in DDIM using just a few steps and get quick renders and then switch to a higher number of steps or switch to a sampler with more steps as you move towards a final image. There can be a general difference in rendering speed from one sampler to another. These samplers tend to all render at about the same speed. These samplers all render slower than those other samplers but these all run at about the same speed as each other. In general, I think it's safe to pick a sampler to work with and use it at a moderate number of steps between 15 and 20. I'm working with 20 here. When you get an image working to your liking then fiddle with changing the steps or the sampler and see if you get something else you like. Again, I had that one image that I like. I changed samplers and I find another image that I like. That's great, I've got both of them. I've saved them both. I've got the parameters for both. I can recreate and iterate from either of them from here. To make this choice of steps and samplers and making comparisons easier, Stable Diffusion offers a cool tool which we'll look at next.

#### [Changing SD models](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/changing-sd-models?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/changing-sd-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of [[Stable Diffusion]]'s advantages over other AI imaging systems like [[Midjourney]] and [[DALL-E]] is that you can change its underlying data model. The data model is everything that it learned during its training. Everything that it knows about how to render anything in the world you can swap that out for a different understanding. This allows you to change the style of its renderings, it allows you to get it to render specialized content and generally completely change the way that it sees the world. Whether you can change the data model or not depends a lot on which instance of Stable Diffusion you're using. Here in Mage.Space, if I click this View Options button, the first thing that comes up is a choice of models. So by default, I'm on the Stable Diffusion 1.5 model, I can switch to the 2.1 model, and if I pay money, I get access to all of these other things. And Mage.Space is constantly adding more models. It's a curated collection. They're choosing them for specific reasons. One thing that's nice about their selection is there's not a lot of redundancy. You won't have 12 different models that are tailored towards rendering anime, for example. Other Stable Diffusion instances may or may not have an option to install other models. If you're running Automatic 1111, then you can choose to install more models on your own. The first thing you got to do is find one. [HuggingFace.co](https://HuggingFace.co) is a good place to start. [[Hugging Face]] is just a good Stable Diffusion resource in general, if you click here on the Models tab, you'll get a long list of possible models.
>

On some models, you might also see something called Checkpoint or CKPT. Checkpoint is the original model format. The problem with a checkpoint file is that it is possible for there to be malicious content in a code that could be executed. So most people are switching these days to SafeTensor or PickleTensor format. Either of these are fine with Automatic 1111. #### [Using embeddings](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/using-embeddings?u=76281980)

What these do is not introduce new data to the model, but rather they go in at the text level, and they add a token that causes you to navigate to a particular point in the data space of your model. #### [Upscaling SD images](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/upscaling-sd-images?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/upscaling-sd-images?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've already discussed how [[Stable Diffusion]]'s rendering sizes are pretty small by any modern standard. Even if you don't intend to print, 512 by 512 is still a low pixel count even for phone viewing. But image size is not only a concern for viewing and output. One reason that we're getting messed up faces is because with the compositions we've been making, the faces are small in the frame. There just aren't a lot of pixels there to work with and so the AI gets confused. Upscaling is a necessary step for correcting the fine details in our images. Of course, you can throw a rendering into Photoshop or another image editor and resize it there and probably get pretty good results. These tools work by analyzing the pixels in the frame to figure out which pixels should be duplicated to create a bigger image. This approach does not consider the content of the image at all. If only there was some kind of tool that understood image content. Oh wait. Most instances of Stable Diffusion will include some kind of scaling tool. These tools actually bring AI training to bear on the problem of upscaling and they can deliver very good results. Scaling controls and options vary a lot from distribution to distribution. For example, here in mage.space, I've got the normal prompt that we've been working with. I'm going to ask it to render that. And when it's done, I get this. I really like this image. You're going to see the word latent a lot when talking about [[Stable Diffusion]]. What it's referring to is the latent image data. Now, if you're coming from a film background this will make sense to you, because when you speak of a negative, a film negative, you think of it as having a latent image in it. There's an image in there, it just needs to be brought out. All of that weird noise data that's in our model,
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/changing-aspect-ratios-with-img2img?u=76281980&t=284)** that's latent image data. It's waiting to be realized. It's waiting to be sampled into something. So what this is saying is that it's going to resize the canvas and then it is going to use a latent image rendering process to try to fill this in. So this isn't looking too much different to me than the just resize option. It's kept my whole composition but it's distorted the person. So none of these options are working great. Now, where you can get in trouble here is when you are dragging and dropping an image from somewhere else. So by default, if I come into image to image, I'm at 512 by 512. If I drop an image in here with a different aspect ratio, it does not update these. So now, if I try to make an image to image change, things are going to get messed up because the original image is this aspect ratio. But image to image is being asked to generate a square aspect ratio. So if I ask it to do something, I've lost my original aspect ratio.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/changing-aspect-ratios-with-img2img?u=76281980&t=377)** Now you can of course find out the original aspect ratio by dragging this into the PNG info tab. #### [Outpainting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/outpainting?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/outpainting?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You've seen how inpainting can be used to add and remove details within an image. We are now going to add details outside of the image, a process called outpainting. Let's say [[Microsoft Word|word]] has come down from on high that some changes have been made to the layout and now this needs to be a rectangular image. It needs to have a landscape aspect ratio. This particular image lives where it lives because it has 512 by 512 pixels. Different width is going to change the data enough that I'm going to get a different image. I want to keep the astronomer in the middle of the frame. So I'm going to start by switching to my inpainting model. And then I'm going to come down here and fire up my poor man's outpainting script which defaults to adding 128 pixels to all four sides. I want only the left and right side. Even though they both need the same settings, even though they both need the same prompt, it's just better to do them separately. So I'm going to set this to only the left side. I want the new content that's generated to be filled or the new area that's generated to be filled with latent noise so that the content will be derived from that. But again, playing with, I'm not adjusting any of my denoising strength or CFG sliders so there's a lot that I can do there. Make him a woman. None of these things I'm doing are stacking because I am not moving this image into img2img. It's done that. Her face is a little messed up. But again, we could in paint that later. So very easy way to rip through a lot of changes to an image very, very quickly. To install it in AUTOMATIC1111, all you have to do is install the Instruct pix2pix model in your model's folder, just like you would any other model that you download.

> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/instruct-pix2pix?u=76281980&t=518)** I'll include a link to where to get that model down below. #### [OpenPose in ControlNet](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/openpose-in-controlnet?u=76281980)

#### [Limitations using OpenPose](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/limitations-using-openpose?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/limitations-using-openpose?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[ControlNet]] with OpenPose is probably going to become a pretty regular part of your [[Stable Diffusion]] process, but it does have some limitations. Let's take a look at some places where ControlNet maybe will fall down. I'm going to come in here and bring in a model that I want to use as a ControlNet source. So this is something I just asked Stable Diffusion for a man running. I'm going to set my pre-processor to OpenPose and my model to OpenPose. Now you've seen how if I enter a prompt and generate a rendering, I will get my final rendering and I will get a second image that shows what the pose was. If I want to preview any of the images that ControlNet derives through a pre-processor, I can just say Preview Annotator Result. If I click that, it shows me what it's going to pull from my image and feed as an input to the generation process. So here you can see a typical ControlNet skeleton. There are very specific points that it tracks. Wrist, elbow, shoulder, knee, ankle, hip joint. The first thing it does and the most critical joint of all is this one, the neck, where the neck connects to the shoulders and to the spine. The thing to know about ControlNet is that if it is missing one joint, it can't render anything that is connected further down that chain.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/limitations-using-openpose?u=76281980&t=94)** So if I don't have the shoulders present in an image, I can't use ControlNet. For example, this is another image that came up when I asked for a shot of a guy running on a track. If I pull this over here and ask ControlNet to show me what it sees in terms of OpenPose, it comes up with nothing because he has no shoulders.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/limitations-using-openpose?u=76281980&t=122)** Getting closer in, we can see that ControlNet also tracks the head. This is a point that I can only assume is the middle of the head so maybe the nose. These are definitely the eyes and then it tracks ears. So it can't see my left ear, but it can see my right ear. If you switch to OpenPose Hand as a pre-processor, it will add additional tracking points for hands. This is a fantastic solution sometimes for rendering hands. The problem is it couldn't decipher this one. I've got two fingers and a thumb visible here and it's just seeing it as a single curved shape. So even though OpenPose can't solve this problem, this is still interesting as a diagnostic tool to realize, "Oh, I'm not going to get a good rendering of a hand out of ControlNet here. Maybe I want to go back reposition my hand and shoot this image again." Here's an example of where you can run into some trouble with some of these limitations. This was our final rendered composite yesterday. Let's say we gave this to an art director and they'd say, "I'd rather he was a woman." Okay, well let's drop it over here into ControlNet. We've got our OpenPose model running. But if we look at the pre-processor, we see it's pretty confused here because a lot of his limbs are hidden. It has decided to stick a leg down here that we actually can't see in this frame.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/limitations-using-openpose?u=76281980&t=218)** What that means is if I try to regenerate this frame using the same prompt, I'm getting really scrambled results because it doesn't know how to reconcile this extra leg. So it's going to be difficult for me to get back to that exact same composition. This might be a case where I want to go into img2img and use my instruct-pix2pix model and ask it to change him to a woman. That might be the only way that I can get a really clean transition to making him female.
