> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/choosing-a-controlnet-model?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In [[ControlNet]], this pre-processor menu and the corresponding model menu can be a little intimidating because there are so many options here. I can tell you right away that you don't need to understand any of the [[Algorithms]] that are going on here and for the most part, you don't need to have some kind of general universal idea about most of these models. I say most of them because there are some of these models that are intended for specific purposes and we've already seen that. Scribble is for hand drawn images, hand drawn scribbles. OpenPose is for extracting poses. MLSD actually has an important use. I'm going to grab another image out here and drop it into ControlNet. And if I switch to MLSD and the MLSD model, I don't actually have to do this just to preview. MLSD looks for straight lines. That's all it does. Doesn't matter if they're vertical, horizontal, or diagonal. It finds all the straight lines in your image. This is great for architecture, for interior spaces, for closeups of geometric models. Look what it did with the trees here. It didn't even bother to try to figure these out. MLSD does not do organic shapes at all but if you are trying to use ControlNet on something with a lot of strong vertical lines, it's going to be a great model. For the most part, most of these other things are more general purpose. So how do you choose? Let's go back. I'm tired of looking at it but let's go back to this shot of me. This is a source frame from the rotoscoping that you saw in the movie at the end of chapter two.

> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/choosing-a-controlnet-model?u=76281980&t=94)** I wanted through ControlNet to process each frame to stylize it, to make it look different, but it was critical that my mouth appeared to stay in sync with my voice and that proved to be a little bit tricky. In the last movie we did this rendering which came out pretty good. Obviously I haven't dialed in any stylization. I did all that through the prompt but here's what Canny came up with in terms of edge detection. Can you really tell what's my mouth and what's not? In this image, it's actually a pretty good match. You can tell that my mouth is open but in a lot of images I ended up with just a closed mouth. "O" sounds and other vowels that left my mouth circular also seemed to really confuse Canny so all I did was go through some other models and look at what they extracted from them. Depth map works by shading things that are closer in a lighter tone than things that are far away. A street scene where there's a car in the foreground in a building in the background, that might benefit a lot from a depth map. This doesn't because I'm interested in getting details in my mouth and they're not here. So that was obviously not going to be a good choice. > **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/choosing-a-controlnet-model?u=76281980&t=188)** that my mouth doesn't have super fine detail but it's very separate from the mustache and beard and so [[Stable Diffusion]] had an easier time figuring out the shapes when I was using the HED processor. I wanted the processor to have more to play with. Edges in an image are a function of contrast. The reason you can see an edge right here is because this is darker than this. So I lit this scene. I put two lights, one over here and one over here and if you look closely, you can see that this side of my face is lighter than that side of my face. That gives me contour, but it makes edges or in this case, wrinkles show up more.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/choosing-a-controlnet-model?u=76281980&t=282)** I did not put a backlight because I don't have room in my apartment to set up a white backdrop far enough away to work with a backlight. But if you are trying to create imagery to use as a source for ControlNet, know that you're going to get better results from your pre-processor if you've got a lot of contrast in the image and you control contrast through lighting. I didn't have the exposure on my camera nailed for this so I actually processed all these frames in Photoshop before I sent them here. I brightened them up a little and added a little bit of contrast there. When you are trying to choose a model, simply go by trial and error. There are going to be some things that you know, openpose for poses, scribbles for scribbles, MLSD for things with lots of straight lines. I think for most organic shapes, HED might be a better choice a lot of the time than Canny because Canny produces such a noisy texture. For foliage and stuff with lots of fine detail, Canny might be the way to go. NormalMap is more useful if you're working with content coming out of 3D rendering software. Some of these others, honestly, I have not played with yet because I haven't needed them. In fact, you don't need to install all of these if you want to save yourself the download and if you want to have a ControlNet menu that's not so confusing. For the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of this course, you're going to see me probably moving through either Canny, HED, and of course I'll be using openpose and maybe scribble. Otherwise, choosing a model is just dependent
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/choosing-a-controlnet-model?u=76281980&t=376)** on what your image is and what result you're going for.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Image size and ControlNet](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/image-size-and-controlnet?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/image-size-and-controlnet?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Once you've installed [[ControlNet]] in Automatic1111, you'll find yourself with all sorts of size controls. There's the normal width and height that you have up here in text-to-image, then you get down here into ControlNet and there's resize mode and canvas width and height. Fortunately, this is all much easier than it seems. As we've seen, the first thing that ControlNet does when it runs is to create this annotator result. This is the edge detail that it will feed into the system to help it render an image. And because you can use one image to influence another image or influence a scene that you've specked out up here, it's possible that this image is a different size and aspect ratio than what you've specked out up here or than what you're using if you're working in the image-to-image tab. These resize modes simply control how this image is resized to match whatever your size specifications are up here. Or if you're in image-to-image, whatever your size specifications are there. You have three options. You can tell it to just resize it to match. You can tell it to scale this to fit whatever is specified up here. Or you can go the other way. Honestly, I don't know that there's a huge difference in any of these. I just leave it on just resize and I usually get exactly what I want. In theory, it's possible that scaling in one direction or another will introduce artifacts that will mess things up. That level of artifact-ing is so small compared to like getting a hand with six fingers
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/image-size-and-controlnet?u=76281980&t=92)** that I just don't really worry about it that much. These two controls here are specifying the size of a blank canvas that will get created when you click the create blank canvas button. And if you notice, if I go to a new ControlNet node, it says drop image here or click to upload. If I say create blank canvas, now I've got drawing tools and so you can see this is a square. I'm going to erase that. Come down here. Change this to be wider. Say create blank canvas. And now, I've got a canvas that is wider than it is tall. So I can scribble right in here, switch to my scribble pre-processor and model, and I'm ready to go. So that's all that these are for. Again, they're very simple. Choose any one of these. You're probably going to be fine. If you are finding strange artifacts in some of your ControlNet renderings, you could consider adjusting these if there's a big difference between what you've put into your ControlNet image and what you're trying to do up here. All of these same controls exist in ControlNet in image-to-image and they work the same way.

#### [Other features in ControlNet](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/other-features-in-controlnet?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/other-features-in-controlnet?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] I want to very quickly go over some of the other things that are scattered around the [[ControlNet]] interface that we haven't talked about, and which honestly you're probably not going to use, but I don't want you to feel like I'm hiding something from you. So up here we've got this image button. If I click on that, I get a standard open dialogue, so I can grab an image, that's as opposed to dragging and dropping like I've been doing. If I've got a camera on the device that I'm using, I can fire it up and shoot an image right in here. Invert input color and RGB to BGR. These have to do with the fact that if you want, you can skip this pre-processor thing and generate an edge map on your own using some other kind of software. Edge detection is a standard part of sharpening [[Algorithms]] and lots of other things that you'll find in your image editor. Or if you're generating material in a 3D program, you may have the ability to create normal maps and depth maps on your own. If so, they need to be, they may need to be inverted or they may be in a color mode that needs to be altered. Another way to put it is these are advanced options that you're probably never going to use. Guess Mode at the time of this shooting is an experimental feature that is meant to allow you to use ControlNet without a prompt in text to image it's, we'll try to guess at the image. I fiddled with it a little bit and it does a pretty good job. It's also easy enough to write a prompt. Guidance start and guidance end are here in the same place as weight because they also allow you to control
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/other-features-in-controlnet?u=76281980&t=93)** how much influence ControlNet is going to have over your image. And what they let you do is tell it within your step count, sampling step count, when will ControlNet step in and start working and when will it step out? So right now ControlNet is going to be influencing every single step. I'm dialed in for 20 steps right now, so every one of them will get ControlNet as an input. This goes from zero to one, so this is a percentage. So if I put it at 25 or 0.25, a quarter of the way in, ControlNet will start. Until then, [[Stable Diffusion]] will be left on its own. Similarly, I can control when it comes out. The thinking behind this is that the bulk of what happens in an image gets settled in the first few steps. Some big decisions are made by the AI, and then it sticks with those and refines them. This allows you to have ControlNet back off from those initial steps so that you're working more with CFG Scale or in the case of image to image with De-noising strength to get some good strong elements settled. And then ControlNet comes in and works on the details. Conversely, you could set ControlNet to zero so that it's working right away and then set the guidance out here so that it steps out so that it lets these controls have more influence over the details. We're not going to go into examples of this here. This is an advanced concept that's related to something similar you can do with prompt writing where you can have prompts have more weight at the beginning or end of the process. We'll be covering those in a future course.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/other-features-in-controlnet?u=76281980&t=186)** And again, if you're wondering why your ControlNet only shows one control model tab, that's because you need to turn on additional tabs by going to settings and then into ControlNet and adjusting the multi ControlNet max models slider. As you've seen, we're getting away fine with just two. I can't imagine you would ever need more than three.

You can change to another model, learn how to drive that. Soon you can have a real toolbox full of different models for different looks and different styles.

>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/creating-models-with-dreambooth?u=76281980&t=281)** if you make a model and you're not happy with the results, there are really only two things that you should work with unless you're going to try to dig into this and really learn how all this works. The first is training steps per image or Epochs, which defaults to 100 and we get some, some help here. This is the total number of training steps that will be performed on each instance image. This is just like the steps in txt2img or image to image. This is how many passes it's going to take, adding noise and analyzing what happened. To high number, which is good. We're only giving it 20 images. We want to really feed it a lot, as much useful data as we can. It is possible to overtrain a model. You might say, "Well, of a 100 is good, I don't mind giving up some time. Let's set it to a 1,000." If you overtrain the model, then the references that you're creating to the thing that you are trying to model may not work. Basically the AI is going to be thinking too much and it's just going to lose track of how to find whatever it is that you're trying to add. The other thing that you would want to fiddle with if you feel like you're not pleased with the results of your model is where to go down here learning rate. It defaults to 0.000002. So if you do find that, well, I'd like to see if I can get better quality, I'm going to change the learning rate. Don't say I'll set it to 50 or something like that. Plainly, this is an extremely granular adjustment here. Maybe you bump it up to 0.000003. Don't do big changes here and on a fast card it's half an hour to find
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/creating-models-with-dreambooth?u=76281980&t=374)** out what the change does. So adjusting these is fiddly and some trial and error and I would definitely recommend doing some reading on what the various settings here do and whether or not they're really going to solve the problems you think you're having. Two other things you could fiddle with if you do have a slower GPU or a GPU with less memory, you could set save model frequency and save preview frequency to zero. These are kind of feedback and safety mechanisms. They don't impact the overall quality of your model and they theoretically slow down the generation process. It takes my high end high ram GPU 25 to 30 minutes to create a model. If changing these knocks off two minutes, I don't care. So I just leave those. So let's move on to the concepts tab. There is stuff here that has to be filled out. This should be a path to your source images. Now, this is a little bit tricky. The documentation says that I can drag any path in here that I want. That has not been my experience with this version of Dreambooth. I'm on my Mac right now, but I'm connected to my [[Windows]] machine. That's where Dreambooth is running on that server. If I drag in a path to a directory on my Mac, I get an error when I try to train the model. And it's not an error about the dataset directory it's an error about concepts needing to be filled out. I fiddled with this for a long time before I finally moved my images over to my Windows machine and tried doing this there.
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/creating-models-with-dreambooth?u=76281980&t=467)** It still didn't work. Finally it worked when I put it inside the Dreambooth folder that's inside the models folder, that's inside my [[Stable Diffusion]] web UI folder. Once I moved my images into there and copied and pasted that path into the dataset directory field, then things were working. I'm not actually going to start the training process that you're going to see right now. We're just going to look at how to fill it out. The other two critical things to complete here are under training prompts instance prompt. We want to put an example of how we would reference the thing that we're trying to add. So I'm going to say photo of, I need to choose something here that isn't probably already in the model, the six gigabyte model of everything in the world. So I should use my full name or a nonsense version of my name or something. What I'm going to choose is mememe. And if I'm not feeling confident about that I can add a fourth me. Obviously, it's critical that I remember what this reference is. It's really worth writing things these things down in a notepad somewhere. Next is a class. What is the thing that I'm rendering? I could say it's a person. I could say it's a man. I could say I'm a being, whatever. I'm going to say person. And those are the only critical things that I need to fill out. Again, if you're going to do a lot of this and you've got a fast video card, this is a great way to go when Dreambooth is working. Hopefully things are going to settle down. Again, it's open source things are moving quickly in the Automatic 1111 world right now. There's no reason to think that the Dreambooth problems we've been having are going to continue. But again, if you don't have a fast video card if you don't think you're going to do that much modeling or maybe even if you are you just don't want to hassle with figuring things out on your own, you can stick with [astria.ai](https://astria.ai).

#### [Merging models](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/merging-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/merging-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Automatic1111 has a built-in feature called Checkpoint Merger, which allows you to combine models. So, if you watched Star Trek TV shows or movies in the '80s and '90s, you know that a Klingon has a particular look. The model Klingon diffusion lets you render images of Klingons in [[Stable Diffusion]]. So, I'm going to say, I've called up the model Klingon diffusion. I have to use the [[Microsoft Word|word]] keyword Klingon. So, I'm going to say Klingon in the desert, and I end up with pretty good Klingons, got all the weird brow ridge stuff and the hair, and they even give me a lot of Klingon clothing and weapons and things that, because it's Stable Diffusion, when they're holding them, they point in funny directions. These look like images that actually could come from a Star Trek show 'cause it's a Klingon in the desert. But I can also say Klingon in a shopping mall, and it does a pretty good job. I'm going to take the default name that it creates and then I have this multiplier. This determines how much the resulting model is going to pull from my primary model and how much it's going to pull from the secondary model. If I set it halfway, I will get a model that's half me, half Klingon. You can see here it says set to 0 to get model A. I can tell you right now that if I use this setting, it's not going to work. And I believe the reason it doesn't work is that the Klingon diffusion model was trained with lots of pictures of Klingons. Klingon in this model is very strong. If I use the Klingon keyword, I always get a Klingon. It never gets confused and gives me just a dark-skinned person, with a wrinkled brow. It's always a good, solid Klingon. The model of me was trained with 22 images. When I'm using it on its own, it's possible for it to screw up sometimes and not give me what looks like me. It'll gimme some other guy with gray hair. So when I do a 50/50 merge, it's basically like the Klingon diffusion model is the dominant gene. Everything comes out Klingon. I can't get anything that looks remotely like me. So, I'm going to say sks man as a Klingon in the desert. So, it gave me Klingon clothes, it gave me Klingon boots, it gave me a Klingon thing.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/merging-models?u=76281980&t=281)** It's done a good job with the desert, but it's just kept me. it was smart enough to gimme sunglasses and it it squared them off a little bit so they look a little more Klingon. So, I'm sure you can see the huge value of being able to merge yourself with Klingons. But Checkpoint Merger has some other uses as well. >
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/merging-models?u=76281980&t=375)** and [[Hugging Face]] are mergers that people have come up with. They fine tune them by very carefully setting the multiplier and maybe adding some additional material. You'll often see in the description references to source models that were used and combined to produce whatever you're looking at. Easy to do, quick to render. Checkpoint Mergers are definitely something to play with.

### Conclusion

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What's next](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-next?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-next?u=76281980&t=0)** - I've authored a lot of courses here at [[LinkedIn]] Learning, and I have to say that this was one of the most difficult simply because [[Stable Diffusion]] is changing so quickly. When I started writing this course, [[ControlNet]] did not exist, which means Openpose editing did not exist and so on and so forth. Stable Diffusion is, and will remain, a moving target for anyone who's trying to learn how to master it. The good news is that what we've covered in this course likely will not change. While some tools may be replaced by others, the underlying neural net structure will likely stay constant for a long time, meaning that most of what you've learned here will stay relevant. What might change? For sure, Stable Diffusion's official data models will continue to improve, and those improvements will include better photorealism, better ability to render text, maybe the ability to render vector-style graphics, and maybe most importantly, the ability for people out in the world to have their images removed from the source training set. In addition to being more fair to creators, this also removes the threat of Stable Diffusion going away because of legal action around this issue of the rights associated with training images. My recommendation to you if you want to stay up to speed with Stable Diffusion is to subscribe to some good YouTube channels or other news sources that stay on top of new developments and take a look at them from time to time. Stable Diffusion's creators would definitely like the two series models to be the source of all future development, so it's safe to assume that a Version 3.0 Series will have more in common with two series models
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/stable-diffusion-tips-tricks-and-techniques/what-s-next?u=76281980&t=95)** than with 1.5 models. I've stressed using 1.5 models in this course, but it would be in your long-term best interest to gain some familiarity with two series models, so that you will be better prepared for what comes next. That said, the bulk of the user community development in Stable Diffusion is around 1.5 models, so the cool models will probably continue to use 1.5 as their basis for a long time to come. As with all creative endeavors, the best way to get better at Stable Diffusion is through practice. Hopefully, you've already seen that it gets easier to use the longer you use it, and so you will understand when I say, "Stop watching me and go start making some images!"

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Stable Diffusion]] (8), [[LinkedIn]] (1), [[ControlNet]] (1)
> **Versions:** 1.5 (4), version 3 (1)
> **Speakers:** - i (1)
